<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma_physics">Plasma physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Plasma physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The field of <a href="/wiki/Plasma_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Plasma physics">plasma physics</a> deals with phenomena of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic</a> nature that involve very high temperatures. It is customary to express temperature as energy in units of <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">electronvolts</a> (eV) or kiloelectronvolts (keV). The energy, which has a different <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">dimension</a> from temperature, is then calculated as the product of the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> and temperature, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>E</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>k</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>B</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mi>T</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E=k_{B}T}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/69bbfc34c34c496184265a64f50bc8d07e4eee94" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:9.25ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="E=k_{B}T" /></span>. Then, 1 eV corresponds to <span style="white-space:nowrap">11<span style="margin-left:0.25em">605</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em">K</span></span>. In the study of <a href="/wiki/QCD_matter" title="QCD matter">QCD matter</a> one routinely encounters temperatures of the order of a few hundred <a href="/wiki/MeV" class="mw-redirect" title="MeV">MeV</a>, equivalent to about <span style="white-space:nowrap">10<sup>12</sup><span style="margin-left:0.25em">K</span></span>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theoretical_foundation">Theoretical foundation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Theoretical foundation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">Thermodynamic temperature</a></div>
<p>Historically, there are several scientific approaches to the explanation of temperature: the classical thermodynamic description based on macroscopic empirical variables that can be measured in a laboratory; the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">kinetic theory of gases</a> which relates the macroscopic description to the probability distribution of the energy of motion of gas particles; and a microscopic explanation based on <a href="/wiki/Statistical_physics" title="Statistical physics">statistical physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. In addition, rigorous and purely mathematical treatments have provided an axiomatic approach to classical thermodynamics and temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-caratheodory_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caratheodory-61">[61]</a></sup> Statistical physics provides a deeper understanding by describing the atomic behavior of matter, and derives macroscopic properties from statistical averages of microscopic states, including both classical and quantum states. In the fundamental physical description, using <a href="/wiki/Natural_units" title="Natural units">natural units</a>, temperature may be measured directly in units of energy. However, in the practical systems of measurement for science, technology, and commerce, such as the modern <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric system</a> of units, the macroscopic and the microscopic descriptions are interrelated by the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>, a proportionality factor that scales temperature to the microscopic mean kinetic energy.</p>
<p>The microscopic description in <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanics</a> is based on a model that analyzes a system into its fundamental particles of matter or into a set of classical or <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum-mechanical</a> oscillators and considers the system as a <a href="/wiki/Statistical_ensemble_(mathematical_physics)" title="Statistical ensemble (mathematical physics)">statistical ensemble</a> of <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">microstates</a>. As a collection of classical material particles, temperature is a measure of the mean energy of motion, called <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, of the particles, whether in solids, liquids, gases, or plasmas. The kinetic energy, a concept of <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>, is half the <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of a particle times its <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speed</a> squared. In this mechanical interpretation of thermal motion, the kinetic energies of material particles may reside in the velocity of the particles of their translational or vibrational motion or in the inertia of their rotational modes. In monatomic <a href="/wiki/Perfect_gas" title="Perfect gas">perfect gases</a> and, approximately, in most gases, temperature is a measure of the mean particle kinetic energy. It also determines the probability distribution function of the energy. In condensed matter, and particularly in solids, this purely mechanical description is often less useful and the oscillator model provides a better description to account for quantum mechanical phenomena. Temperature determines the statistical occupation of the microstates of the ensemble. The microscopic definition of temperature is only meaningful in the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_limit" title="Thermodynamic limit">thermodynamic limit</a>, meaning for large ensembles of states or particles, to fulfill the requirements of the statistical model.</p>
<p>In the context of thermodynamics, the kinetic energy is also referred to as <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a>. The thermal energy may be partitioned into independent components attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degrees of freedom</a> of the particles or to the modes of oscillators in a <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">thermodynamic system</a>. In general, the number of these degrees of freedom that are available for the <a href="/wiki/Equipartition_theorem" title="Equipartition theorem">equipartitioning</a> of energy depend on the temperature, i.e. the energy region of the interactions under consideration. For solids, the thermal energy is associated primarily with the <a href="/wiki/Atom_vibrations" title="Atom vibrations">vibrations</a> of its atoms or molecules about their equilibrium position. In an <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal monatomic gas</a>, the kinetic energy is found exclusively in the purely translational motions of the particles. In other systems, <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibrational</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotational</a> motions also contribute degrees of freedom.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinetic_theory_of_gases">Kinetic theory of gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Kinetic theory of gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Translational_motion.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/Translational_motion.gif" width="300" height="263" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="263" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Translational_motion.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A theoretical understanding of temperature in an ideal gas can be obtained from the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">Kinetic theory</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">Maxwell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">Boltzmann</a> developed a <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">kinetic theory</a> that yields a fundamental understanding of temperature in gases.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> This theory also explains the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a> law and the observed heat capacity of <a href="/wiki/Monatomic_gas" title="Monatomic gas">monatomic</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">'noble'</a>) gases.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-K.26K_391_397_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K.26K_391_397-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_thermometer_and_absolute_zero.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Gas_thermometer_and_absolute_zero.jpg/220px-Gas_thermometer_and_absolute_zero.jpg" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Gas_thermometer_and_absolute_zero.jpg/330px-Gas_thermometer_and_absolute_zero.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Gas_thermometer_and_absolute_zero.jpg/440px-Gas_thermometer_and_absolute_zero.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1114" data-file-height="608" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_thermometer_and_absolute_zero.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Plots of pressure vs temperature for three different gas samples extrapolated to absolute zero.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_law" title="Ideal gas law">ideal gas law</a> is based on observed <a href="/wiki/Empirical_relationship" title="Empirical relationship">empirical relationships</a> between pressure (<i>p</i>), volume (<i>V</i>), and temperature (<i>T</i>), and was recognized long before the kinetic theory of gases was developed (see <a href="/wiki/Boyle%27s_law" title="Boyle's law">Boyle's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles%27s_law" title="Charles's law">Charles's</a> laws). The ideal gas law states:<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>p</mi>
        <mi>V</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>n</mi>
        <mi>R</mi>
        <mi>T</mi>
        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />
        <mspace width="negativethinmathspace" />
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle pV=nRT\,\!}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eeb01fbc11b96a10b11abf1c8b72cbd36d9b8c94" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.079ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:11.379ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="pV=nRT\,\!" /></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>n</i> is the number of <a href="/wiki/Mole_unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Mole unit">moles</a> of gas and <i>R</i>&#160;=&#160;<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000831445980000000♠</span>8.314<span style="margin-left:.25em;">4598</span>(48)&#160;J⋅mol<sup>−1</sup>⋅K<sup>−1</sup></span><sup id="cite_ref-physconst-R_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physconst-R-67">[67]</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/Gas_constant" title="Gas constant">gas constant</a>.</p>
<p>This relationship gives us our first hint that there is an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a> on the temperature scale, because it only holds if the temperature is measured on an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_temperature" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolute temperature">absolute</a> scale such as Kelvins. The <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_law" title="Ideal gas law">ideal gas law</a> allows one to measure temperature on this <a href="/wiki/Absolute_temperature" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolute temperature">absolute</a> scale using the <a href="/wiki/Gas_thermometer" title="Gas thermometer">gas thermometer</a>. The temperature in kelvins can be defined as the pressure in pascals of one mole of gas in a container of one cubic meter, divided by the gas constant.</p>
<p>Although it is not a particularly convenient device, the <a href="/wiki/Gas_thermometer" title="Gas thermometer">gas thermometer</a> provides an essential theoretical basis by which all thermometers can be calibrated. As a practical matter it is not possible to use a gas thermometer to measure absolute zero temperature since the gases tend to condense into a liquid long before the temperature reaches zero. It is possible, however, to extrapolate to absolute zero by using the ideal gas law, as shown in the figure.</p>
<p>The kinetic theory assumes that pressure is caused by the force associated with individual atoms striking the walls, and that all energy is translational <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>. Using a sophisticated symmetry argument,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">Boltzmann</a> deduced what is now called the <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">Maxwell–Boltzmann probability distribution</a> function for the velocity of particles in an ideal gas. From that <a href="/wiki/Probability_distribution" title="Probability distribution">probability distribution</a> function, the average <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, <i>E<sub>k</sub></i> (per particle), of a <a href="/wiki/Monatomic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monatomic">monatomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a> is:<sup id="cite_ref-K.26K_391_397_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K.26K_391_397-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mi>E</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mtext>k</mtext>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mn>1</mn>
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mi>m</mi>
        <msubsup>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">r</mi>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
            </mrow>
          </mrow>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msubsup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mn>3</mn>
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mi>k</mi>
        <mi>T</mi>
        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />
        <mo>,</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E_{\text{k}}={\frac {1}{2}}mv_{\mathrm {rms} }^{2}={\frac {3}{2}}kT\,,}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b1f554255d102b01e271630250edba049585d1de" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:23.087ex; height:5.176ex;" alt="E_{\text{k}}={\frac {1}{2}}mv_{\mathrm {rms} }^{2}={\frac {3}{2}}kT\,," /></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>, <var style="padding-right: 1px;">k</var>, is the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_constant" class="mw-redirect" title="Ideal gas constant">ideal gas constant</a> divided by the <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_number" class="mw-redirect" title="Avogadro number">Avogadro number</a>, and <i>v<sub>rms</sub></i> is the <a href="/wiki/Root-mean-square_speed" title="Root-mean-square speed">root-mean-square speed</a>. Thus the ideal gas law states that <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a> is directly proportional to temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-atkins_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atkins-70">[70]</a></sup> This direct proportionality between temperature and <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a> is a special case of the <a href="/wiki/Equipartition_theorem#General_formulation_of_the_equipartition_theorem" title="Equipartition theorem">equipartition theorem</a>, and holds only in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical</a> limit of an <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a>. It does not hold for most substances, although it is true that temperature is a <a href="/wiki/Monotonic_function" title="Monotonic function">monotonic</a> (non-decreasing) function of <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeroth_law_of_thermodynamics">Zeroth law of thermodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Zeroth law of thermodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zeroth_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Zeroth law of thermodynamics">Zeroth law of thermodynamics</a></div>
<p>When two otherwise isolated bodies are connected together by a rigid physical path impermeable to matter, there is spontaneous transfer of energy as heat from the hotter to the colder of them. Eventually they reach a state of mutual <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a>, in which heat transfer has ceased, and the bodies' respective state variables have settled to become unchanging.</p>
<p>One statement of the <a href="/wiki/Zeroth_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Zeroth law of thermodynamics">zeroth law of thermodynamics</a> is that if two systems are each in thermal equilibrium with a third system, then they are also in thermal equilibrium with each other.</p>
<p>This statement helps to define temperature but it does not, by itself, complete the definition. An empirical temperature is a numerical scale for the hotness of a thermodynamic system. Such hotness may be defined as existing on a <a href="/wiki/Manifold#Motivational_examples" title="Manifold">one-dimensional manifold</a>, stretching between hot and cold. Sometimes the zeroth law is stated to include the existence of a unique universal hotness manifold, and of numerical scales on it, so as to provide a complete definition of empirical temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-Serrin_1978_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serrin_1978-54">[54]</a></sup> To be suitable for empirical thermometry, a material must have a monotonic relation between hotness and some easily measured state variable, such as pressure or volume, when all other relevant coordinates are fixed. An exceptionally suitable system is the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a>, which can provide a temperature scale that matches the absolute Kelvin scale. The Kelvin scale is defined on the basis of the second law of thermodynamics.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_law_of_thermodynamics">Second law of thermodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Second law of thermodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">Second law of thermodynamics</a></div>
<p>In the previous section certain properties of temperature were expressed by the zeroth law of thermodynamics. It is also possible to define temperature in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> which deals with <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>. The second law states that any process will result in either no change or a net increase in the entropy of the universe. This can be understood in terms of probability.</p>
<p>For example, in a series of coin tosses, a perfectly ordered system would be one in which either every toss comes up heads or every toss comes up tails. This means that for a perfectly ordered set of coin tosses, there is only one set of toss outcomes possible: the set in which 100% of tosses come up the same. On the other hand, there are multiple combinations that can result in disordered or mixed systems, where some fraction are heads and the rest tails. A disordered system can be 90% heads and 10% tails, or it could be 98% heads and 2% tails, et cetera. As the number of coin tosses increases, the number of possible combinations corresponding to imperfectly ordered systems increases. For a very large number of coin tosses, the combinations to ~50% heads and ~50% tails dominates and obtaining an outcome significantly different from 50/50 becomes extremely unlikely. Thus the system naturally progresses to a state of maximum disorder or entropy.</p>
<p>It has been previously stated that temperature governs the transfer of heat between two systems and it was just shown that the universe tends to progress so as to maximize entropy, which is expected of any natural system. Thus, it is expected that there is some relationship between temperature and entropy. To find this relationship, the relationship between heat, work and temperature is first considered. A <a href="/wiki/Heat_engine" title="Heat engine">heat engine</a> is a device for converting thermal energy into mechanical energy, resulting in the performance of work, and analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Carnot_heat_engine" title="Carnot heat engine">Carnot heat engine</a> provides the necessary relationships. The work from a heat engine corresponds to the difference between the heat put into the system at the high temperature, <i>q<sub>H</sub></i> and the heat ejected at the low temperature, <i>q<sub>C</sub></i>. The efficiency is the work divided by the heat put into the system or:</p>

<p><a href="/wiki/Carnot%27s_theorem_(thermodynamics)" title="Carnot's theorem (thermodynamics)">Carnot's theorem</a> states that all reversible engines operating between the same heat reservoirs are equally efficient. Thus, a heat engine operating between <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>3</sub> must have the same efficiency as one consisting of two cycles, one between <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>2</sub>, and the second between <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>3</sub>. This can only be the case if:</p>

<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >

<p>Since the first function is independent of <i>T</i><sub>2</sub>, this temperature must cancel on the right side, meaning <i>f</i>(<i>T</i><sub>1</sub>,<i>T</i><sub>3</sub>) is of the form <i>g</i>(<i>T</i><sub>1</sub>)/<i>g</i>(<i>T</i><sub>3</sub>) (i.e. <i>f</i>(<i>T</i><sub>1</sub>,<i>T</i><sub>3</sub>) = <i>f</i>(<i>T</i><sub>1</sub>,<i>T</i><sub>2</sub>)<i>f</i>(<i>T</i><sub>2</sub>,<i>T</i><sub>3</sub>) = <i>g</i>(<i>T</i><sub>1</sub>)/<i>g</i>(<i>T</i><sub>2</sub>)· <i>g</i>(<i>T</i><sub>2</sub>)/<i>g</i>(<i>T</i><sub>3</sub>) = <i>g</i>(<i>T</i><sub>1</sub>)/<i>g</i>(<i>T</i><sub>3</sub>)), where <i>g</i> is a function of a single temperature. A temperature scale can now be chosen with the property that:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {q_{C}}{q_{H}}}={\frac {T_{C}}{T_{H}}}\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,(6)}</annotation>

</dl>
<p>For <i>T<sub>C</sub></i> = 0 K the efficiency is 100% and that efficiency becomes greater than 100% below 0 K. Since an efficiency greater than 100% violates the first law of thermodynamics, this implies that 0 K is the minimum possible temperature. In fact the lowest temperature ever obtained in a macroscopic system was 20 nK, which was achieved in 1995 at NIST. Subtracting the right hand side of Equation 5 from the middle portion and rearranging gives:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >

<p>where the subscript indicates a reversible process. The change of this state function around any cycle is zero, as is necessary for any state function. This function corresponds to the entropy of the system, which was described previously. Rearranging Equation 6 gives a new definition for temperature in terms of entropy and heat:</p>

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T={\frac {dq_{\mathrm {rev} }}{dS}}\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,(9)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/45edf723a1f5c6560566f46a90f5c1dc8dcf3e25" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:59.43ex; height:5.509ex;" alt="T={\frac {dq_{\mathrm {rev} }}{dS}}\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,\,(9)" /></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>For a system, where entropy <i>S</i>(<i>E</i>) is a function of its energy <i>E</i>, the temperature <i>T</i> is given by:</p>

</dl>
<p>i.e. the reciprocal of the temperature is the rate of increase of entropy with respect to energy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_from_statistical_mechanics">Definition from statistical mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Definition from statistical mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">Statistical mechanics</a> defines temperature based on a system's fundamental degrees of freedom. Eq.(10) is the defining relation of temperature. Eq. (9) can be derived from the principles underlying the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_thermodynamic_relation" title="Fundamental thermodynamic relation">fundamental thermodynamic relation</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalized_temperature_from_single-particle_statistics">Generalized temperature from single-particle statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Generalized temperature from single-particle statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is possible to extend the definition of temperature even to systems of few particles, like in a <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">quantum dot</a>. The generalized temperature is obtained by considering time ensembles instead of configuration-space ensembles given in statistical mechanics in the case of thermal and particle exchange between a small system of <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> (<i>N</i> even less than 10) with a single/double-occupancy system. The finite quantum <a href="/wiki/Grand_canonical_ensemble" title="Grand canonical ensemble">grand canonical ensemble</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-finense_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finense-71">[71]</a></sup> obtained under the hypothesis of <a href="/wiki/Ergodicity" title="Ergodicity">ergodicity</a> and orthodicity,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> allows to express the generalized temperature from the ratio of the average time of occupation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

          <mi>&#x03C4;<!-- τ --></mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>1</mn>

        </msub>

<p>where <i>E</i><sub>F</sub> is the <a href="/wiki/Fermi_energy" title="Fermi energy">Fermi energy</a>. This generalized temperature tends to the ordinary temperature when <i>N</i> goes to infinity.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Negative_temperature">Negative temperature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Negative temperature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Negative_temperature" title="Negative temperature">Negative temperature</a></div>
<p>On the empirical temperature scales, which are not referenced to absolute zero, a negative temperature is one below the zero-point of the scale used. For example, <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a> has a sublimation temperature of <span style="white-space:nowrap">−78.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em">°C</span></span> which is equivalent to <span style="white-space:nowrap">−109.3<span style="margin-left:0.25em">°F</span></span>. On the absolute Kelvin scale, however, this temperature is 194.6 K. On the absolute scale of thermodynamic temperature no material can have a temperature smaller than or equal to 0 K, both of which are forbidden by the <a href="/wiki/Third_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Third law of thermodynamics">third law of thermodynamics</a>.</p>
<p>Temperature is basically defined for a body in its own state of internal thermodynamic equilibrium, and in this definition, on an absolute scale, it is always positive. In an apparent contradiction of this reliable and valid rule, a so-called negative absolute "temperature" may be approximately defined for a component of a body that is not in its own state of internal thermodynamic equilibrium: a component may have a negative approximate "temperature" while the rest of the components of the body have positive approximate temperatures. Such a non-equilibrium situation is either transient in time or is maintained by external factors that drive a flow of energy through the body of interest. An example of such a component is a spin system within a body, as follows.</p>
<p>In the quantum mechanical description of electron and nuclear spin systems that have a limited number of possible states, and therefore a discrete upper limit of energy they can attain, it is possible to obtain a <a href="/wiki/Negative_temperature" title="Negative temperature">negative temperature</a>, which is numerically indeed less than absolute zero. However, this is not the macroscopic temperature of the material, but instead the temperature of only very specific degrees of freedom, that are isolated from others and do not exchange energy by virtue of the <a href="/wiki/Equipartition_theorem" title="Equipartition theorem">equipartition theorem</a>.</p>
<p>A negative temperature is experimentally achieved with suitable radio frequency techniques that cause a <a href="/wiki/Population_inversion" title="Population inversion">population inversion</a> of spin states from the ground state. As the energy in the system increases upon population of the upper states, the entropy increases as well, as the system becomes less ordered, but attains a maximum value when the spins are evenly distributed among ground and excited states, after which it begins to decrease, once again achieving a state of higher order as the upper states begin to fill exclusively. At the point of maximum entropy, the temperature function shows the behavior of a <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_singularity" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematical singularity">singularity</a>, because the slope of the entropy function decreases to zero at first and then turns negative. Since temperature is the inverse of the derivative of the entropy, the temperature formally goes to infinity at this point, and switches to negative infinity as the slope turns negative. At energies higher than this point, the spin degree of freedom therefore exhibits formally a negative thermodynamic temperature. As the energy increases further by continued population of the excited state, the negative temperature approaches zero asymptotically.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> As the energy of the system increases in the population inversion, a system with a negative temperature is not colder than absolute zero, but rather it has a higher energy than at positive temperature, and may be said to be in fact hotter at negative temperatures. When brought into contact with a system at a positive temperature, energy will be transferred from the negative temperature regime to the positive temperature region.</p>

<div class="plainlist">
<ul>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_waterA"><a href="#ref_waterA"><b><sup>A</sup></b></a></span> For <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Standard_Mean_Ocean_Water" title="Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water">Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water</a> at one standard atmosphere (101.325&#160;kPa) when calibrated strictly per the two-point definition of thermodynamic temperature.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_incandescentB"><a href="#ref_incandescentB"><b><sup>B</sup></b></a></span> The 2500&#160;K value is approximate. The 273.15&#160;K difference between K and °C is rounded to 300&#160;K to avoid <a href="/wiki/False_precision" title="False precision">false precision</a> in the Celsius value.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_tungestenC"><a href="#ref_tungestenC"><b><sup>C</sup></b></a></span> For a true black-body (which tungsten filaments are not). Tungsten filament emissivity is greater at shorter wavelengths, which makes them appear whiter.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_sunD"><a href="#ref_sunD"><b><sup>D</sup></b></a></span> Effective photosphere temperature. The 273.15&#160;K difference between K and °C is rounded to 273&#160;K to avoid false precision in the Celsius value.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_celsiuskelvinE"><a href="#ref_celsiuskelvinE"><b><sup>E</sup></b></a></span> The 273.15&#160;K difference between K and °C is within the precision of these values.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_zmachineF"><a href="#ref_zmachineF"><b><sup>F</sup></b></a></span> For a true black-body (which the plasma was not). The Z machine's dominant emission originated from 40&#160;MK electrons (soft x-ray emissions) within the plasma.</li>
</ul>
</div>

Mech</i>. <b>1</b>: P01003. <a href="/wiki/ArXiv" title="ArXiv">arXiv</a>:<span class="plainlinks"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//arxiv.org/abs/1001.2342">1001.2342</a><span style="margin-left:0.1em"><img alt="Freely accessible" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png" title="Freely accessible" width="9" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/14px-Lock-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/18px-Lock-green.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="813" /></span></span>. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2010JSMTE..01..003P">2010JSMTE..01..003P</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1088%2F1742-5468%2F2010%2F01%2FP01003">10.1088/1742-5468/2010/01/P01003</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATemperature&amp;rft.atitle=The+finite+quantum+grand+canonical+ensemble+and+temperature+from+single-electron+statistics+for+a+mesoscopic+device&amp;rft.au=Prati%2C+E.&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iop.org%2FEJ%2Fabstract%2F1742-5468%2F2010%2F01%2FP01003%2F&amp;rft_id=info%3Aarxiv%2F1001.2342&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2010JSMTE..01..003P&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1088%2F1742-5468%2F2010%2F01%2FP01003&amp;rft.jtitle=J.+Stat.+Mech.&amp;rft.pages=P01003&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=1" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://arxiv.org/abs/1001.2342v1">arxiv.org</a></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Charles_Kittel" title="Charles Kittel">Kittel, Charles</a>; <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kroemer" title="Herbert Kroemer">Kroemer, Herbert</a> (1980). <i>Thermal Physics</i> (2nd ed.). W. H. Freeman Company. pp.&#160;Appendix E. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7167-1088-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-7167-1088-9">0-7167-1088-9</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATemperature&amp;rft.aufirst=Charles&amp;rft.au=Kroemer%2C+Herbert&amp;rft.aulast=Kittel&amp;rft.btitle=Thermal+Physics&amp;rft.date=1980&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-7167-1088-9&amp;rft.pages=Appendix+E&amp;rft.pub=W.+H.+Freeman+Company&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The cited emission wavelengths are for black bodies in equilibrium. CODATA 2006 recommended value of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6997289776850000000♠</span>2.897<span style="margin-left:.25em;">7685</span>(51)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−3</sup>&#160;m K</span> used for Wien displacement law constant <i>b</i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-ltl-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ltl_76-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ltl.tkk.fi/wiki/LTL/World_record_in_low_temperatures">"World record in low temperatures"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2009-05-05</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATemperature&amp;rft.btitle=World+record+in+low+temperatures&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fltl.tkk.fi%2Fwiki%2FLTL%2FWorld_record_in_low_temperatures&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-recordcold-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-recordcold_77-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A temperature of 450&#160;±80&#160;pK in a Bose–Einstein condensate (BEC) of sodium atoms was achieved in 2003 by researchers at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>. Citation: <i>Cooling Bose–Einstein Condensates Below 500 Picokelvin</i>, A. E. Leanhardt <i>et al</i>., Science <b>301</b>, 12 Sept. 2003, p. 1515. It's noteworthy that this record's peak emittance black-body wavelength of 6,400 kilometers is roughly the radius of Earth.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The peak emittance wavelength of <span style="white-space:nowrap">2.897<span style="margin-left:0.25em">77</span></span>&#160;m is a frequency of 103.456&#160;MHz</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Measurement was made in 2002 and has an uncertainty of ±3 kelvin. A <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.kis.uni-freiburg.de/~hw/astroandsolartitles.html">1989 measurement</a> produced a value of 5,777.0±2.5&#160;K. Citation: <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://theory.physics.helsinki.fi/~sol_phys/Sol0601.pdf"><i>Overview of the Sun</i></a> (Chapter 1 lecture notes on Solar Physics by Division of Theoretical Physics, Dept. of Physical Sciences, University of Helsinki).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The 350&#160;MK value is the maximum peak fusion fuel temperature in a thermonuclear weapon of the Teller–Ulam configuration (commonly known as a <i>hydrogen bomb</i>). Peak temperatures in Gadget-style fission bomb cores (commonly known as an <i>atomic bomb</i>) are in the range of 50 to 100&#160;MK. Citation: <i>Nuclear Weapons Frequently Asked Questions, 3.2.5 Matter At High Temperatures.</i> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://nuclearweaponarchive.org/Nwfaq/Nfaq3.html#nfaq3.2">Link to relevant Web page.</a> All referenced data was compiled from publicly available sources.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Peak temperature for a bulk quantity of matter was achieved by a pulsed-power machine used in fusion physics experiments. The term <i>bulk quantity</i> draws a distinction from collisions in particle accelerators wherein high <i>temperature</i> applies only to the debris from two subatomic particles or nuclei at any given instant. The &gt;2&#160;GK temperature was achieved over a period of about ten nanoseconds during <i>shot Z1137</i>. In fact, the iron and manganese ions in the plasma averaged 3.58±0.41&#160;GK (309±35&#160;keV) for 3&#160;ns (ns 112 through 115). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://prl.aps.org/abstract/PRL/v96/i7/e075003"><i>Ion Viscous Heating in a Magnetohydrodynamically Unstable Z Pinch at Over <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7009200000000000000♠</span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>9</sup></span> Kelvin</i></a>, M. G. Haines <i>et al.</i>, Physical Review Letters <b>96</b> (2006) 075003. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://sandia.gov/news-center/news-releases/2006/physics-astron/hottest-z-output.html">Link to Sandia's news release.</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Core temperature of a high–mass (&gt;8–11 solar masses) star after it leaves the <i>main sequence</i> on the <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung-Russell_diagram" class="mw-redirect" title="Hertzsprung-Russell diagram">Hertzsprung–Russell diagram</a> and begins the <i><a href="/wiki/Alpha_reactions" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpha reactions">alpha process</a></i> (which lasts one day) of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_burning_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Silicon burning process">fusing silicon–28</a> into heavier elements in the following steps: sulfur–32 → argon–36 → calcium–40 → titanium–44 → chromium–48 → iron–52 → nickel–56. Within minutes of finishing the sequence, the star explodes as a Type&#160;II <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a>. Citation: <i>Stellar Evolution: The Life and Death of Our Luminous Neighbors</i> (by Arthur Holland and Mark Williams of the University of Michigan). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://umich.edu/~gs265/star.htm">Link to Web site</a>. More informative links can be found here <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://schools.qps.org/hermanga/images/Astronomy/chapter_21___stellar_explosions.htm">[2]</a>, and here <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://cosserv3.fau.edu/~cis/AST2002/Lectures/C13/Trans/Trans.html">[3]</a>, and a concise treatise on stars by NASA is&#160;here <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://nasa.gov/worldbook/star_worldbook.html">[4]</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090116111858/http://umich.edu/~gs265/star.htm">Archived</a> January 16, 2009, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Based on a computer model that predicted a peak internal temperature of 30&#160;MeV (350&#160;GK) during the merger of a binary neutron star system (which produces a gamma–ray burst). The neutron stars in the model were 1.2 and 1.6 solar masses respectively, were roughly 20&#160;km in diameter, and were orbiting around their barycenter (common center of mass) at about 390&#160;Hz during the last several milliseconds before they completely merged. The 350&#160;GK portion was a small volume located at the pair's developing common core and varied from roughly 1 to 7&#160;km across over a time span of around 5&#160;ms. Imagine two city-sized objects of unimaginable density orbiting each other at the same frequency as the G4 musical note (the 28th white key on a piano). It's also noteworthy that at 350 GK, the average neutron has a vibrational speed of 30% the speed of light and a relativistic mass (<i>m</i>) 5% greater than its rest mass (<i>m</i><sub>0</sub>).&#160; <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://arxiv.org/pdf/astro-ph/0507099.pdf"><i>Torus Formation in Neutron Star Mergers and Well-Localized Short Gamma-Ray Bursts</i></a>, R. Oechslin <i>et al</i>. of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mpa-garching.mpg.de/">Max Planck Institute for Astrophysics.</a>, arXiv:astro-ph/0507099 v2, 22 Feb. 2006. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mpa-garching.mpg.de/mpa/research/current_research/hl2005-10/hl2005-10-en.html">An html summary</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Results of research by Stefan Bathe using the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.phenix.bnl.gov/">PHENIX</a> detector on the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bnl.gov/rhic/">Relativistic Heavy Ion Collider</a> at <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bnl.gov/world/">Brookhaven National Laboratory</a> in Upton, New York, U.S.A.&#160; Bathe has studied gold-gold, deuteron-gold, and proton-proton collisions to test the theory of quantum chromodynamics, the theory of the strong force that holds atomic nuclei together.&#160; <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://bnl.gov/bnlweb/pubaf/pr/PR_display.asp?prID=06-56">Link to news release.</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://public.web.cern.ch/public/Content/Chapters/AboutCERN/HowStudyPrtcles/HowSeePrtcles/HowSeePrtcles-en.html">How do physicists study particles?</a> by <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://public.web.cern.ch/public/Welcome.html">CERN</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Planck frequency equals <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7043185487000000000♠</span>1.854<span style="margin-left:.25em;">87</span>(14)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>43</sup>&#160;Hz</span> (which is the reciprocal of one Planck time). Photons at the Planck frequency have a wavelength of one Planck length. The Planck temperature of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7032141679000000000♠</span>1.416<span style="margin-left:.25em;">79</span>(11)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>32</sup>&#160;K</span> equates to a calculated <i>b&#160;</i>/<i>T</i>&#160;=&#160;λ<sub><i>max</i></sub> wavelength of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6965204531000000000♠</span>2.045<span style="margin-left:.25em;">31</span>(16)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−26</sup>&#160;nm</span>. However, the actual peak emittance wavelength quantizes to the Planck length of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6965161624000000000♠</span>1.616<span style="margin-left:.25em;">24</span>(12)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−26</sup>&#160;nm</span>.</span></li>
</ol>
